Affected individuals typically have either neuromuscular or skeletal manifestations, although overlapping phenotypes can occur. There is a wide range of phenotypic severity.             <div class="main-content lit-style" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/CreativeWork"><div class="meta-content fm-sec"><h1 id="_NBK201366_"><span class="title" itemprop="name">Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>Related Disorders</span></h1><p class="contrib-group"><span itemprop="author">Brett A McCray</span>, MD, PhD, <span itemprop="author">Alice Schindler</span>, MS, CGC, <span itemprop="author">Julie E Hoover-Fong</span>, MD, PhD, and <span itemprop="author">Charlotte J Sumner</span>, MD.</p><a data-jig="ncbitoggler" href="#__NBK201366_ai__" style="border:0;text-decoration:none">Author Information and Affiliations</a><div style="display:none" class="ui-widget" id="__NBK201366_ai__"><div class="contrib half_rhythm"><span itemprop="author">Brett A McCray</span>, MD, PhD<div class="affiliation small">Department of Neurology<br />University of Michigan Medical School<br />Ann Arbor, Michigan<div><span class="email-label">Email: </span><a href="mailto:dev@null" data-email="ude.hcimu.dem@yarccmb" class="oemail">ude.hcimu.dem@yarccmb</a></div></div></div><div class="contrib half_rhythm"><span itemprop="author">Alice Schindler</span>, MS, CGC<div class="affiliation small">National Institute of Neurological Disorders and Stroke, Neurogenetics Branch<br />National Institutes of Health<br />Bethesda, Maryland<div><span class="email-label">Email: </span><a href="mailto:dev@null" data-email="vog.hin.liam@bareldnihcs" class="oemail">vog.hin.liam@bareldnihcs</a></div></div></div><div class="contrib half_rhythm"><span itemprop="author">Julie E Hoover-Fong</span>, MD, PhD<div class="affiliation small">Department of Genetic Medicine<br />Johns Hopkins University School of Medicine<br />Baltimore, Maryland<div><span class="email-label">Email: </span><a href="mailto:dev@null" data-email="ude.imhj@2revoohj" class="oemail">ude.imhj@2revoohj</a></div></div></div><div class="contrib half_rhythm"><span itemprop="author">Charlotte J Sumner</span>, MD<div class="affiliation small">Departments of Neurology, Neuroscience, and Genetic Medicine<br />Johns Hopkins University School of Medicine<br />Baltimore, Maryland<div><span class="email-label">Email: </span><a href="mailto:dev@null" data-email="ude.imhj@1renmusc" class="oemail">ude.imhj@1renmusc</a></div></div></div></div><p class="small">Initial Posting: <span itemprop="datePublished">May 15, 2014</span>; Last Update: <span itemprop="dateModified">December 4, 2025</span>.</p><p><em>Estimated reading time: 43 minutes</em></p></div><div class="jig-ncbiinpagenav body-content whole_rhythm" data-jigconfig="allHeadingLevels: ['h2'],smoothScroll: false" itemprop="text"><div id="cmt2c.Summary" itemprop="description"><h2 id="_cmt2c_Summary_">Summary</h2><div><h4 class="inline">Clinical characteristics.</h4><p>The <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related disorders include two principal types: neuromuscular disorders and skeletal dysplasias. Affected individuals typically have either neuromuscular or skeletal manifestations, although overlapping phenotypes can occur. There is a wide range of phenotypic severity. In the mildest of the autosomal dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>related disorders life span is normal; in the most severe it is shortened.</p><p>The three clinically recognized <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> neuromuscular disorders are Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type 2C, scapuloperoneal spinal muscular atrophy, and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a> distal spinal muscular atrophy. Clinical overlap between these phenotypes does occur. These neuromuscular disorders are characterized by a motor-predominant, non-length-dependent peripheral neuropathy or motor neuronopathy with a wide range in age of onset, from congenital to late adult. Additional common features include laryngeal dysfunction (i.e., vocal fold paresis causing voice change and/or inspiratory stridor), diaphragm weakness (orthopnea), scoliosis, bilateral sensorineural hearing loss, and joint contractures.</p><p>The five <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> skeletal dysplasias are (from mildest to most severe) <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> digital arthropathy with brachydactyly, brachyolmia, spondylometaphyseal dysplasia (Kozlowski type), spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia (Maroteaux type), and metatropic dysplasia. All <i>TRPV4</i>-related skeletal dysplasias are characterized by brachydactyly; the four most severe forms have short stature that varies from mild to severe with progressive spinal deformity and involvement of the long bones and pelvis.</p></div><div><h4 class="inline">Diagnosis/testing.</h4><p>The diagnosis of an <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related disorder is established in a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/proband/">proband</a> who has characteristic clinical and neurophysiologic findings, radiographic findings in the skeletal dysplasias, and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> <i>TRPV4</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> identified by <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/molecular-genetic-testing/">molecular genetic testing</a>.</p></div><div><h4 class="inline">Management.</h4><p><i>Treatment of manifestations:</i> Treatment is focused on symptom management. Affected individuals are often evaluated and managed by a multidisciplinary team that may include neurologists, physiatrists, orthopedic surgeons, otologists/audiologists, laryngologists, pulmonologists, geneticists, and physical and occupational therapists. This multidisciplinary team will determine the best treatment options for supportive care, prevention of obesity, and management of pain and depression.</p><p>For neuromuscular disorders, additional treatment includes special shoes with good ankle support, shoe orthotics, ankle-foot orthoses&#x000a0;/ knee-ankle-foot orthoses, surgery for severe foot deformities, mobility devices, and exercise as tolerated; management of kyphoscoliosis per orthopedist; management of tethered spinal cord per neurosurgeon; laryngeal surgery for vocal cord paresis (in some individuals, vocal fold lateralization or tracheostomy); speech therapy; and respiratory therapy including noninvasive ventilatory support as needed.</p><p>For skeletal dysplasias, additional treatment includes physical therapy/exercise and heel cord stretching to maintain function; surgical intervention when kyphoscoliosis compromises pulmonary function and/or causes pain and/or when upper cervical spine instability and/or cervical myelopathy are present.</p><p><i>Surveillance:</i> For neuromuscular disorders, annual neurologic examination, physical therapy assessment, otolaryngology evaluation of laryngeal function, dynamic breathing chest radiograph, pulmonary function tests, sleep study, hearing assessment, and musculoskeletal evaluation; assessment of weight, height, and weight-for-height or body composition to diagnose obesity at each visit. For skeletal dysplasias, annual evaluation for joint pain and scoliosis; assessment for odontoid hypoplasia before a child reaches school age and before surgical procedures involving general anesthesia; annual hearing assessment; and assessment of weight, height, and weight-for-height at each visit.</p><p><i>Agents/circumstances to avoid:</i> Obesity, as it makes walking more difficult. For neuromuscular disorders, avoid diabetes and neurotoxic medications; upper respiratory tract infections can cause vocal fold swelling and worsen upper airway obstruction. For skeletal dysplasias, avoid extreme neck flexion and extension (in those with odontoid hypoplasia); activities that place undue stress on the spine and weight-bearing joints.</p><p><i>Pregnancy management:</i> Ideally a woman with an <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related disorder would seek consultation from a high-risk obstetrician or maternal-fetal medicine specialist to evaluate risk associated with pregnancy and delivery.</p></div><div><h4 class="inline">Genetic counseling.</h4><p>By definition, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related disorders are inherited in an autosomal dominant manner. Because the most severe <i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal phenotypes can be lethal in childhood (or in utero), children with these phenotypes typically have a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/de-novo/"><i>de novo</i></a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> and unaffected parents. Individuals with less severe skeletal phenotypes or neuromuscular phenotypes often have the disorder as the result of a <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variant inherited from a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> parent (<i>de novo</i> pathogenic variants have also been described in individuals with these phenotypes). Each child of an individual with an autosomal dominant <i>TRPV4</i>-related disorder has a 50% chance of inheriting the <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variant. Specific <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a>, age of onset, and disease severity cannot be accurately predicted because of reduced <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/penetrance/">penetrance</a> and highly <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/variable-expressivity/">variable expressivity</a>. However, in general, a child who inherits a <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variant associated with neuromuscular disease or skeletal dysplasia from an affected parent is likely to have a similar overall phenotype as the parent, although the age of onset and severity may be quite distinct. Once the <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variant has been identified in an affected family member, prenatal and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/preimplantation-genetic-testing/">preimplantation genetic testing</a> are possible.</p></div></div><div id="cmt2c.GeneReview_Scope"><h2 id="_cmt2c_GeneReview_Scope_"><i>GeneReview</i> Scope</h2><div id="cmt2c.Tc" class="table"><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.Tc/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__cmt2c.Tc_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.Tc_1_1_1_1" colspan="2" scope="colgroup" rowspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>Related Disorders: Included Phenotypes&#x000a0;<sup>1</sup></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.Tc_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Neuromuscular disorders</b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.Tc_1_1_1_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type 2, TRPV4-related (CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Scapuloperoneal spinal muscular atrophy, TRPV4-related</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Congenital distal spinal muscular atrophy, TRPV4-related</div></li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.Tc_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Skeletal dysplasias</b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.Tc_1_1_1_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Familial digital arthropathy with brachydactyly, <i>TRPV4</i>-related</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Brachyolmia, <i>TRPV4-</i>related</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Spondylometaphyseal dysplasia, <i>TRPV4-</i>related (Kozlowski type)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (Maroteaux type)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Metatropic dysplasia, <i>TRPV4</i>-related</div></li></ul>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">For synonyms and outdated names see <a href="#cmt2c.Nomenclature">Nomenclature</a>.</p></div></dd><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.c.1"><p class="no_margin">The phenotypes comprising the two groups of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related disorders are listed from mildest to most severe.</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Diagnosis"><h2 id="_cmt2c_Diagnosis_">Diagnosis</h2><div id="cmt2c.Suggestive_Findings"><h3>Suggestive Findings</h3><div id="cmt2c.Neuromuscular_Disorders"><h4>Neuromuscular Disorders</h4><p>An <b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related neuromuscular disease should be suspected</b> in individuals with the following clinical findings based on <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> (see <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTneurologicfindingsbytrpv4neu">Table 1</a>) and family history.</p><p>
<b>Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type 2, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (CMT2C)</b>
</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>A progressive peripheral neuronopathy/neuropathy (primarily motor rather than sensory) associated with bilateral pes cavus, distal amyotrophy, and foot drop. Weakness can be non-length dependent and asymmetrical, affecting proximal muscles in the arms (shoulder abduction weakness and scapular winging) and legs (asymmetric weakness of knee extension and hip flexion). When present, sensory loss affects vibration more than pain sensation [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>].</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Nerve conduction studies [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>] show (1) reduced compound motor action potential (CMAP) amplitudes with normal velocities (&#x0003e;40-60 m/s) and (2) normal, decreased, or absent distal sensory nerve action potential (SNAP) amplitudes.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Electromyography shows predominantly chronic neurogenic changes.</div></li></ul></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Laryngeal dysfunction (i.e., vocal fold paresis) that may be bilateral and severe (resulting in inspiratory stridor and/or a raspy [hoarse] voice) or asymmetric (often more severe on the left than the right). Laryngoscopy often shows paresis of one or both vocal folds [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Sensorineural hearing loss (SNHL) in some individuals that is typically bilateral, progressive, and mild to moderate. Onset is from childhood to adulthood [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.landour_.2010.170">Landour&#x000e9; et al 2010</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Respiratory dysfunction in some individuals due to diaphragm muscle weakness, which may lead to respiratory insufficiency, orthopnea, and/or sleep apnea [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.chen.2010.1968">Chen et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. Central sleep apnea has also been described [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.ragamin.2022.305">Ragamin et al 2022</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.taga.2022.375">Taga et al 2022</a>]. Chest radiograph and pulmonary function tests may demonstrate diaphragm weakness with decreased inspiratory and expiratory pressures.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Kyphoscoliosis [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Joint contractures primarily affecting the legs (ankles, knees, and hips). When severe, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> can appear similar to arthrogryposis multiplex congenita (AMC), although this is much more common in <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a> distal spinal muscular atrophy, <i>TRPV4</i>-related.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Short stature in some individuals</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Bladder dysfunction, most commonly incontinence and urinary frequency [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Skin manifestations can occur, including scaliness, dryness, itching, and fissures [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]</div></li></ul><p>
<b>Scapuloperoneal spinal muscular atrophy, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (<i>TRPV4-</i>SPSMA)</b>
</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Slowly progressive lower motor neuron loss associated with muscle weakness and atrophy proximally in the shoulder girdle region (with characteristic scapular winging) and distally in the peroneal (lower leg) muscles, although proximal arm and leg muscles can be affected. The <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> is clinically and phenotypically similar to CMT2C but with the absence of sensory involvement. In severe <i>TRPV4-</i>SPSMA, absence of muscle and weakness are evident at birth, thus appearing clinically similar to <i>TRPV4</i>-related <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a> distal spinal muscular atrophy.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Muscle biopsy (infrequently performed) shows evidence of denervation and renervation [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.deng.2010.165">Deng et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berciano.2011.1413">Berciano et al 2011</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Laryngeal dysfunction (laryngomalacia and vocal fold anomalies as in CMT2C), vocal cord paresis, and transient dysphonia [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berciano.2011.1413">Berciano et al 2011</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>SNHL (as in CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Respiratory dysfunction (as in CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Kyphoscoliosis (as in CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Joint contractures (as in CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Bladder dysfunction (as in CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Skin manifestations (as in CMT2C)</div></li></ul><p>
<b>Congenital distal spinal muscular atrophy, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (<i>TRPV4</i>-CDSMA)</b>
</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Congenital-onset, non-progressive or slowly progressive lower motor neuron loss associated with muscle weakness and atrophy, predominantly affecting the lower extremities</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Flexion contractures of the knees and hips often present at birth (i.e., AMC). Severe bilateral clubfoot is also seen.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Tethered spinal cord in some individuals, which may not be limited to <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a>-onset forms of disease [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Other features seen in CMT2C and SPSMA (e.g., laryngeal dysfunction, kyphoscoliosis, SNHL, respiratory dysfunction) can occur.</div></li></ul><p><b>Family history</b> is typically consistent with <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> inheritance (e.g., affected males and females in multiple generations), although recessive disease has been described for a single <i>TRPV4</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.velilla.2019.e312">Velilla et al 2019</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.lugo.2023.e43413">Lugo et al 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berth.2025.564">Berth et al 2025</a>]. Individuals with severe or early lethal <i>TRPV4-</i>related phenotypes often have a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/de-novo/"><i>de novo</i></a> pathogenic variant, although severe phenotypes can also occur in families with other affected individuals. Absence of a known family history does not preclude the diagnosis.</p><div id="cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 1. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Neurologic Findings by <i>TRPV4</i> Neuromuscular Phenotype</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" rowspan="2" scope="rowgroup" colspan="1" headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Finding</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2" colspan="3" scope="colgroup" rowspan="1" style="text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;">Phenotype</th></tr><tr><th headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2" id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" colspan="1" scope="colgroup" rowspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">CMT2C</th><th headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2" id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><i>TRPV4</i>-SPSMA</th><th headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2" id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;"><i>TRPV4</i>-CDSMA</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Age at onset</b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Birth to adulthood</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Birth to adulthood</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Prenatal</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">

</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Peripheral, progressive, distal &#x00026; proximal, sensory involvement</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Peripheral, progressive, proximal &#x00026; distal, w/o sensory involvement</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Paresis of legs (&#x00026; pelvic girdle &#x00026; trunk) at birth;&#x000a0;<sup>1</sup> upper extremities variably affected</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Vocal fold</b>
<br />
<b>paresis</b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">+</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">+</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x000b1;</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>SNHL</b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">+</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">+</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x000b1;</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Respiratory</b>
<br />
<b>dysfunction&#x000a0;<sup>2</sup></b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">+</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">+</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x000b1;</td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<b>Joint</b>
<br />
<b>contractures</b>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_1" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x000b1;</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">&#x000b1;</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu_1_1_2_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Common; can include AMC (mainly involving feet, knees, &#x00026; hips)</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">AMC = arthrogryposis multiplex congenita; CDSMA = <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a> distal spinal muscular atrophy; CMT2C = Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type 2C; SNHL = sensorineural hearing loss; SPSMA = scapuloperoneal spinal muscular atrophy</p></div></dd><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.1.1"><p class="no_margin">More mild manifestation: <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a> weakness of the distal part of the lower limbs only. More severe manifestation: weakness of the pelvic girdle and trunk muscles resulting in scoliosis.</p></div></dd><dt>2. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.1.2"><p class="no_margin">Secondary to diaphragmatic involvement</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Skeletal_Dysplasias"><h4>Skeletal Dysplasias</h4><p>An <b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasia should be suspected</b> in individuals with the following skeletal findings and family history.</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Familial digital arthropathy with brachydactyly characterized by the following:</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Normal hands and feet at birth, then relative shortening of the middle and distal phalanges with swelling and decreased range of motion of the interphalangeal joints in early childhood</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Progressive arthropathy of the other joints of the hands and feet with pain and deformity</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>No clinical overlap with other <i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias</div></li></ul></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>The other <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias (brachyolmia, spondylometaphyseal dysplasia [Kozlowski type], spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia [Maroteaux type], and metatropic dysplasia) form a phenotypic continuum of overlapping disorders from mild to severe, each with:</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Short stature;</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Progressive spinal deformity with scoliosis with or without kyphosis and radiographic features of platyspondyly and overfaced pedicles;</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>At least one additional distinctive feature (see <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTautosomaldominanttrpv4related">Table 2</a>).</div></li></ul></li></ul><p><b>Family history</b> is often consistent with <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> inheritance (e.g., affected males and females in multiple generations). Because severe <i>TRPV4-</i>related phenotypes can be lethal in childhood (or in utero), children with these phenotypes typically have a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/de-novo/"><i>de novo</i></a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> and unaffected parents. Absence of a known family history does not preclude the diagnosis.</p><div id="cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 2. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>Related Skeletal Dysplasias: Radiographic and Clinical Features</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a w/waddling gait</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_2_2 hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_3_4" prenatally or perinatally; at birth, short-limb short-stature dwarfism&#x000a0;<sup>4</sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">NA = not applicable</p></div></dd><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.2.1"><p class="no_margin">Overfaced pedicles: lateral border of the vertebrae appears outside the lateral edge of the pedicles, a characteristic feature of <i>TRPV4</i>-related skeletal dysplasias best viewed on AP radiograph of the spine; images in <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.nemec.2012.15">Nemec et al [2012]</a></p></div></dd><dt>2. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.2.2"><p class="no_margin">Histologic findings: thin seal of bone at the chondro-osseous junction, absent primary metaphyseal spongiosa, abnormal metaphyseal vascular invasion, arrest of endochondral ring structures with persistence of circumferential growth</p></div></dd><dt>3. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.2.3"><p class="no_margin">The term "Halberd-shaped pelvis" is derived from the shape of a Swedish battle ax.</p></div></dd><dt>4. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.2.4"><p class="no_margin">Progressive kyphoscoliosis and platyspondyly subsequently alter proportions from short-limb to short-trunk dwarfism.</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Establishing_the_Diagnosis"><h3>Establishing the Diagnosis</h3><p>The diagnosis of an <b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related disorder is established</b> in a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/proband/">proband</a> with <a href="#cmt2c.Suggestive_Findings">suggestive findings</a> and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> pathogenic (or <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a>) variant in <i>TRPV4</i> identified by <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/molecular-genetic-testing/">molecular genetic testing</a> (see <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTautosomaldominanttrpv4related1">Table 3</a>) that is suspected to cause gain of channel function.</p><p>Note: <i>TRPV4</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> p.Ser94Leu has been associated with <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-recessive/">autosomal recessive</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disease (see <a href="#cmt2c.Genetically_Related_Allelic_Disord">Genetically Related Disorders</a>).</p><p>Note: (1) Per American College of Medical Genetics and Genomics / Association for Molecular Pathology variant interpretation guidelines, the terms "<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a>" and "<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a> variant" are synonymous in a clinical setting, meaning that both are considered diagnostic and can be used for clinical decision making [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.richards.2015.405">Richards et al 2015</a>]. Reference to "pathogenic variants" in this <i>GeneReview</i> is understood to include likely pathogenic variants. (2) Identification of a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> <i>TRPV4</i> variant of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/uncertain-significance/">uncertain significance</a> does not establish or rule out the diagnosis.</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>Single-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> testing.</b> Sequence analysis of <i>TRPV4</i> is performed first to detect <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/missense/">missense</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/nonsense-variant/">nonsense</a>, and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/splice-site/">splice site</a> variants and small intragenic deletions/insertions. Note: (1) Depending on the sequencing method used, single-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exon/">exon</a>, multiexon, or whole-gene deletions/duplications may not be detected. (2) To date, a large <i>TRPV4</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion/">deletion</a> or <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/duplication/">duplication</a> has not been reported in an individual with an <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related disorder; all pathogenic variants reported are suspected to result in gain of function.</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div class="half_rhythm"><b>A</b>
<b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/multigene-panel/">multigene panel</a></b> that includes <i>TRPV4</i> and other genes of interest (see <a href="#cmt2c.Differential_Diagnosis">Differential Diagnosis</a>) can be considered to identify the genetic cause of the condition while limiting identification of pathogenic variants and variants of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/uncertain-significance/">uncertain significance</a> in genes that do not explain the underlying <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a>. Note: (1) The genes included in the panel and the diagnostic <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sensitivity/">sensitivity</a> of the testing used for each <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> vary by laboratory and are likely to change over time. (2) Some multigene panels may include genes not associated with the condition discussed in this <i>GeneReview</i>. (3) In some laboratories, panel options may include a custom laboratory-designed panel and/or custom phenotype-focused <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exome/">exome</a> analysis that includes genes specified by the clinician. (4) Methods used in a panel may include <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sequence-analysis/">sequence analysis</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion-duplication-analysis/">deletion/duplication analysis</a>, and/or other non-sequencing-based tests.</div><div class="half_rhythm">For an introduction to multigene panels click <a href="/books/n/gene/app5/#app5.Multigene_Panels">here</a>. More detailed information for clinicians ordering genetic tests can be found <a href="/books/n/gene/app5/#app5.Multigene_Panels_FAQs">here</a>.</div></li></ul><div id="cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 3. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>Related Disorders: Molecular Genetic Testing</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Gene&#x000a0;<sup>1</sup></th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Method</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Proportion of Pathogenic Variants&#x000a0;<sup>2</sup> Identified by Method</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_1" rowspan="2" scope="rowgroup" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">
<i>TRPV4</i>
</td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_2" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Sequence analysis&#x000a0;<sup>3</sup></td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">100%&#x000a0;<sup>4</sup></td></tr><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_2" colspan="1" scope="row" rowspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Deletion/<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/duplication/">duplication</a> analysis&#x000a0;<sup>5</sup></td><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_1_1_1_1_3" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">None reported&#x000a0;<sup>6</sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.3.1"><p class="no_margin">See <a href="/books/NBK201366/#cmt2c.molgen.TA">Table A. Genes and Databases</a> for <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/chromosome/">chromosome</a> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/locus/">locus</a> and protein.</p></div></dd><dt>2. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.3.2"><p class="no_margin">See <a href="#cmt2c.Molecular_Genetics">Molecular Genetics</a> for information on variants detected in this <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a>.</p></div></dd><dt>3. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.3.3"><p class="no_margin">Sequence analysis can detect variants that are benign, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-benign/">likely benign</a>, of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/uncertain-significance/">uncertain significance</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a>, or pathogenic. Variants may include <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/missense/">missense</a>, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/nonsense-variant/">nonsense</a>, and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/splice-site/">splice site</a> variants and small intragenic deletions/insertions; typically, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exon/">exon</a> or whole-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> deletions/duplications are not detected. For issues to consider in interpretation of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/sequence-analysis/">sequence analysis</a> results, click <a href="/books/n/gene/app2/">here</a>.</p></div></dd><dt>4. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.3.4"><p class="no_margin">Data derived from the subscription-based professional view of Human Gene Mutation Database [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.stenson.2020.1197">Stenson et al 2020</a>]</p></div></dd><dt>5. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.3.5"><p class="no_margin">Gene-targeted <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/deletion-duplication-analysis/">deletion/duplication analysis</a> detects intragenic deletions or duplications. Methods used may include a range of techniques such as <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/quantitative-pcr/">quantitative PCR</a>, long-range PCR, multiplex ligation-dependent probe amplification (MLPA), and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a>-targeted microarray designed to detect single-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exon/">exon</a> deletions or duplications.</p></div></dd><dt>6. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.3.6"><p class="no_margin">The authors are unaware of a whole-<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a> or <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/contiguous-gene-deletion/">contiguous gene deletion</a> of <i>TRPV4</i> causing one of the recognized phenotypes.</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Clinical_Characteristics"><h2 id="_cmt2c_Clinical_Characteristics_">Clinical Characteristics</h2><div id="cmt2c.Clinical_Description"><h3>Clinical Description</h3><p>The two groups of disorders and the phenotypes comprising <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related disorders are the following:</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Neuromuscular disorders (See <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.neurologic_findings_by_trpv4_neu/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTneurologicfindingsbytrpv4neu">Table 1</a>.)</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Charcot-Marie-Tooth disease type 2, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (CMT2C)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Scapuloperoneal spinal muscular atrophy, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (<i>TRPV4</i>-SPSMA)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Congenital distal spinal muscular atrophy, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (<i>TRPV4</i>-CDSMA)</div></li></ul></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Skeletal dysplasias, listed from mildest to most severe (See <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTautosomaldominanttrpv4related">Table 2</a>.)</div><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Familial digital arthropathy with brachydactyly, <i>TRPV4</i>-related</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Brachyolmia, <i>TRPV4</i>-related</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Spondylometaphyseal dysplasia, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (Kozlowski type)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia, <i>TRPV4</i>-related (Maroteaux type)</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Metatropic dysplasia, <i>TRPV4</i>-related</div></li></ul></li></ul><p>The phenotypic spectra within both neuromuscular and skeletal groups are broad and overlapping, and the phenotypes of both groups can in rare instances overlap as well [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.taga.2022.375">Taga et al 2022</a>].</p><div id="cmt2c.Neuromuscular_Disorders_1"><h4>Neuromuscular Disorders</h4><p>The <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disorders are peripheral neuropathies/neuronopathies in which motor nerves are more prominently affected than sensory nerves [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. While three distinct clinical phenotypes were originally described (CMT2C, <i>TRPV4</i>-SPSMA, and <i>TRPV4</i>-CDSMA), these phenotypes likely represent an overlapping spectrum of disease and can often not be clearly distinguished [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>].</p><p>Clinical findings and age of onset can be extremely variable in <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disorders both between and within families [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. Affected individuals usually become symptomatic between early childhood and age 25 years; however, disease onset can range from birth, with breathing difficulties and delayed walking, to after the eighth decade [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. In some, the manifestations can be so mild as to go unrecognized by the affected individual and physicians.</p><p>Affected individuals typically demonstrate progressive weakness and atrophy of muscles in the feet and/or hands, usually associated with depressed tendon reflexes and mild or no sensory loss. While weakness is often more severe distally, shoulder girdle weakness and scapular winging are common. Proximal leg weakness can also occur (particularly involving knee extension and hip flexion). Motor manifestations can also be asymmetrical. When present, sensory loss affects vibration more than pain sensation [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. The <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/congenital/">congenital</a> phenotypes, <i>TRPV4</i>-SPSMA and <i>TRPV4</i>-CDSMA, may be characterized by long plateau periods without obvious deterioration [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.vlam.2012.790">Vlam et al 2012</a>], although progressive disease may develop in adulthood.</p><p>Laryngeal dysfunction is a hallmark of <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disease and can occur in each subtype [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.auergrumbach.2010.160">Auer-Grumbach et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.deng.2010.165">Deng et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.landour_.2010.170">Landour&#x000e9; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.zimo_.2010.1798">Zimo&#x00144; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. The severity of motor impairment in the limbs and laryngeal dysfunction do not appear to correlate. Respiratory weakness and sleep apnea are common. Sensorineural hearing loss can also occur.</p><p>Orthopedic manifestations are common and can include joint contractures, hip dysplasia, and scoliosis [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. Tethered spinal cord was reported in 7.5% of a cohort of 40 individuals [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>].</p><p>Individuals with severe features may have a decreased life span secondary to respiratory complications [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.santoro.2002.399">Santoro et al 2002</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.mcentagart.2005.293">McEntagart et al 2005</a>].</p></div><div id="cmt2c.Skeletal_Dysplasias_1"><h4>Skeletal Dysplasias</h4><p><b><i>TRPV4</i>-related <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> digital arthropathy with brachydactyly</b> is not evident at birth because the hands and feet and skeletal examination (including radiographs) are normal [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.hortabaas.2017.512">Horta-Baas et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF._reldemir.2021.46">&#x000dc;rel-Demir et al 2021</a>]. In early childhood relative shortening of the middle and distal phalanges and swelling and decreased range of motion of the interphalangeal joints become apparent. Later, in the first decade and beyond, the other joints of the hands and feet become painful and deformed. No overlap is currently recognized with the manifestations of the other <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related skeletal dysplasias.</p><p>The remaining <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related skeletal disorders are characterized by varying degrees of disproportionate short stature and progressive spinal deformity with scoliosis with or without kyphosis.</p><p><b><i>TRPV4</i>-related brachyolmia</b> is the mildest of the short-stature <i>TRPV4</i>-related skeletal conditions. Its name derives from the Greek roots <i>brachy-</i>, meaning "short," and -<i>olmos</i>, meaning "trunk" or "shoulder." Affected individuals have only mild short stature and the limbs are typically unaffected; thus, physical function is unaffected [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF._reldemir.2021.46">&#x000dc;rel-Demir et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.g_ne_.2025.505">G&#x000fc;ne&#x0015f; et al 2025</a>].</p><p><b><i>TRPV4</i>-related spondylometaphyseal dysplasia (Kozlowski type)</b> is characterized by short-trunk short stature, although the chest is broader than in some of the more severe <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF._reldemir.2021.46">&#x000dc;rel-Demir et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.g_ne_.2025.505">G&#x000fc;ne&#x0015f; et al 2025</a>]. Birth length is average. Affected children usually come to medical attention in early childhood when poor growth with disproportionate stature and a waddling gait with genu varum become evident. Premature osteoarthritis of the joints is common.</p><p><b><i>TRPV4</i>-related spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia (Maroteaux type)</b> is characterized by short-trunk dwarfism and brachydactyly [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF._reldemir.2021.46">&#x000dc;rel-Demir et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.uzman.2023.115">Uzman et al 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.g_ne_.2025.505">G&#x000fc;ne&#x0015f; et al 2025</a>]. Birth length is usually average. Poor growth with a short trunk and overall short stature become evident in childhood. Over time, genu valgum and kyphoscoliosis develop. Osteoporosis has been described.</p><p><b><i>TRPV4</i>-related</b>
<b>metatropic dysplasia</b> (from the Greek <i>metatropos,</i> meaning "with change&#x000a0;/ changing pattern") was named after the striking reversal of body proportions between birth and childhood. At birth, the limbs are disproportionately short (due to the long bone metaphyseal abnormalities) compared to the trunk. In childhood, when the platyspondyly and scoliosis and/or kyphosis become more severe, the trunk becomes relatively short compared to the limbs [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF._reldemir.2021.46">&#x000dc;rel-Demir et al 2021</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.g_ne_.2025.505">G&#x000fc;ne&#x0015f; et al 2025</a>].</p><p>Metatropic dysplasia may be lethal in the prenatal or perinatal period, largely due to an extremely narrow chest and hypoplastic lung parenchyma. Infants who survive the perinatal period typically develop severe kyphoscoliosis that eventually compromises pulmonary function. Other skeletal findings in some individuals with severe metatropic dysplasia are poor joint range of motion, joint contractures, and torticollis; these arthrogryposis multiplex congenita-like contractures represent an overlap between the neuromuscular and skeletal phenotypes of <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV-</i>related disorders [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.unger.2011.2860">Unger et al 2011</a>].</p></div></div><div id="cmt2c.GenotypePhenotype_Correlations"><h3>Genotype-Phenotype Correlations</h3><p>In general, specific sets of <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variants have been associated with either neuromuscular disorders or skeletal dysplasias. However, overlap phenotypes may occur [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.taga.2022.375">Taga et al 2022</a>], making <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/genotype-phenotype-correlations/">genotype-phenotype correlations</a> difficult (see <a href="#cmt2c.Molecular_Genetics">Molecular Genetics</a>). Moreover, pathogenic variants associated with <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disease can cause any of the recognized subtypes, with different presentations even within families.</p><p>Functional studies suggest that <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variants associated with neuromuscular disorders and short-stature skeletal dysplasias cause a gain of channel function [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.rock.2008.999">Rock et al 2008</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.krakow.2009.307">Krakow et al 2009</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.nilius.2013.152">Nilius &#x00026; Voets 2013</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.sullivan.2015.e29">Sullivan et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.woolums.2020.2679">Woolums et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.sullivan.2024.eadk1358">Sullivan et al 2024</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berth.2025.564">Berth et al 2025</a>], whereas there are reports of both <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/loss-of-function/">loss-of-function</a> and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gain-of-function/">gain-of-function</a> features in pathogenic variants associated with <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> digital arthropathy with brachydactyly [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.lamand_.2011.1142">Lamand&#x000e9; et al 2011</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berth.2025.564">Berth et al 2025</a>].</p><p><b><i>TRPV4-</i>related neuromuscular disorders.</b> Several studies suggest that most <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variants associated with a neuromuscular <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> cluster on the highly positively charged convex surface of the ankyrin repeat <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/domain/">domain</a> and target arginine residues that are strictly conserved throughout 27 available <i>TRPV4</i> orthologs [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.auergrumbach.2010.160">Auer-Grumbach et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.deng.2010.165">Deng et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.landour_.2010.170">Landour&#x000e9; et al 2010</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.sullivan.2015.e29">Sullivan et al 2015</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. These surface pathogenic variants are located in three consecutive finger loops of the protein, a distinct region of the <i>TRPV4</i> ankyrin repeat domain [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kwon.2023.3732">Kwon et al 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.nadezhdin.2023.3733">Nadezhdin et al 2023</a>]. The most commonly reported and best validated pathogenic <i>TRPV4</i> variants are <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg186Gln</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg232Cys</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg269Cys</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg269His</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg315Trp</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg316Cys</a>, and <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg316His</a>, although several other pathogenic or <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/likely-pathogenic/">likely pathogenic</a> variants have been reported [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kosmanopoulos.2025.238">Kosmanopoulos et al 2025</a>]. Pathogenic variants in the N-terminal intrinsically disordered region (<a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Ser93Phe</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Pro97Arg</a>) have also been reported [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.fiorillo.2012.195">Fiorillo et al 2012</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berth.2025.564">Berth et al 2025</a>]. Variable phenotypes have been reported, even among members of the same family [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.landour_.2010.170">Landour&#x000e9; et al 2010</a>].</p><p>Note: <i>TRPV4</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a> p.Ser94Leu has been associated with <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-recessive/">autosomal recessive</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disease (see <a href="#cmt2c.Genetically_Related_Allelic_Disord">Genetically Related Disorders</a>).</p><p><b><i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias.</b> In total, more than 50 pathogenic variants in <i>TRPV4</i> have been reported to cause brachyolmias. While the pathogenic variants are spread throughout the <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/gene/">gene</a>, two hot spots have been observed at residues Pro799 in <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/exon/">exon</a> 15 and Arg594 in exon 11 [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.nishimura.2012.190">Nishimura et al 2012</a>], which localize to the channel pore region [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.kwon.2023.3732">Kwon et al 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.nadezhdin.2023.3733">Nadezhdin et al 2023</a>].</p><p>The <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> digital arthropathy with brachydactyly-causing pathogenic variants are restricted to finger 3 of the ankyrin repeats <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/domain/">domain</a> (pathogenic variants <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Gly270Val</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Arg271Pro</a>, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Phe273Leu</a>) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.nilius.2013.152">Nilius &#x00026; Voets 2013</a>].</p><p><b>Overlap of <i>TRPV4-</i>related neuromuscular disorders and skeletal dysplasias.</b> At least 19 pathogenic variants have been associated with both neuromuscular disease and skeletal dysplasia [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.taga.2022.375">Taga et al 2022</a>], with the most common implicated variants being p.Arg269His, <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Ser542Tyr</a>, and <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4_pathogenic_variants_refere/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4pathogenicvariantsrefere">p.Trp785Cys</a>.</p></div><div id="cmt2c.Penetrance"><h3>Penetrance</h3><p><b>Autosomal dominant</b>
<b><i>TRPV4-</i>related neuromuscular disorders.</b> Penetrance is reduced with the neuromuscular disease-associated pathogenic variants.</p><p><b>Autosomal dominant</b>
<b><i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias.</b> In contrast, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/penetrance/">penetrance</a> of the skeletal dysplasia <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> appears to be high; however, intra- and <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/interfamilial-variability/">interfamilial variability</a> is significant [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.dai.2010.704">Dai et al 2010</a>].</p></div><div id="cmt2c.Nomenclature"><h3>Nomenclature</h3><p>Charcot-Marie-Tooth neuropathy type 2C is also referred to as hereditary motor and sensory neuropathy type 2C.</p><p>Spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia (SEMD), <i>TRPV4-</i>related (Maroteaux type), was previously referred to as pseudo-Morquio syndrome type 2.</p><p>Parastremmatic dysplasia is now considered part of the SEMD, Maroteaux type, <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a>.</p></div><div id="cmt2c.Prevalence"><h3>Prevalence</h3><p>The prevalence of the <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4-</i>related neuromuscular and skeletal dysplasias has not been well studied, with estimates ranging from ~1% to 3.5% of Charcot-Marie-Tooth neuropathy type 2 (CMT2)&#x000a0;/ hereditary motor neuropathy (HMN) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.fawcett.2012.1204">Fawcett et al 2012</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.deng.2020.68">Deng et al 2020</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.record.2024.3144">Record et al 2024</a>].</p><p><a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.fawcett.2012.1204">Fawcett et al [2012]</a> determined that 13 (&#x0003c;1%) of 422 individuals with a CMT2 (axonal CMT) <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> were <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/heterozygous/">heterozygous</a> for a <i>TRPV4</i> <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/pathogenic-variant/">pathogenic variant</a>, and <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.record.2024.3144">Record et al [2024]</a> found <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variants in 1.1% of 443 individuals with CMT2/HMN. The detection of a <i>TRPV4</i> pathogenic variant increased to between 9% and 16% in those with a CMT2 phenotype with additional unusual features (e.g., vocal fold weakness, diaphragmatic paresis, skeletal dysplasia) [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.echanizlaguna.2014.1919">Echaniz-Laguna et al 2014</a>].</p></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Genetically_Related_Allelic_Disord"><h2 id="_cmt2c_Genetically_Related_Allelic_Disord_">Genetically Related (Allelic) Disorders</h2><p>Biallelic <i>TRPV4</i> variants have been reported in the following individuals:</p><ul><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Multiple individuals with <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/homozygous/">homozygous</a> p.Ser94Leu pathogenic variants and a <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/phenotype/">phenotype</a> compatible with <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disease have been reported [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.velilla.2019.e312">Velilla et al 2019</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.lugo.2023.e43413">Lugo et al 2023</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berth.2025.564">Berth et al 2025</a>]. This variant showed a partial gain of ion channel function in vitro [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.berth.2025.564">Berth et al 2025</a>].</div></li><li class="half_rhythm"><div>Two sibs were reported with compound partial <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/loss-of-function/">loss-of-function</a> <i>TRPV4</i> variants and complex phenotypes consisting of microcephaly, dystonia, sensorineural hearing loss, retinopathy, skeletal abnormalities, and neuropathy [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.thibodeau.2017.3087">Thibodeau et al 2017</a>]. Further studies would be required to establish pathogenicity of these variants.</div></li></ul></div><div id="cmt2c.Differential_Diagnosis"><h2 id="_cmt2c_Differential_Diagnosis_">Differential Diagnosis</h2><div id="cmt2c.Autosomal_Dominant_TRPV4Related_Ne"><h3>Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4</i>-Related Neuromuscular Disorders</h3><p>Autosomal dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>related neuromuscular disorders resemble several other disorders (see <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTautosomaldominanttrpv4related2">Table 4</a>).</p><p>Note: See <a href="/books/n/gene/cmt/">Charcot-Marie-Tooth Hereditary Neuropathy Overview</a> for a general overview of CMT2.</p><div id="cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 4. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>Related Neuromuscular Disorders: Differential Diagnosis</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Gene</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">MOI</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2_1_1_1_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Phenotype(s)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_2_1_1_1_1" scope="row" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">

<a href="/books/n/gene/sma/">Spinal muscular atrophy</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div><div><dl class="temp-labeled-list small"><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">Genes are listed in alphabetic order.</p></div></dd><dt></dt><dd><div><p class="no_margin">AD = <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a>; ALS = amyotrophic lateral sclerosis; AR = <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-recessive/">autosomal recessive</a>; CMT = Charcot-Marie-Tooth neuropathy; dHMN = distal hereditary motor neuropathy; dSMA = distal spinal muscular atrophy; HMSN = hereditary motor/sensory neuropathy; MOI = <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/mode-of-inheritance/">mode of inheritance</a>; SMA = spinal muscular atrophy; XL = <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/x-linked/">X-linked</a></p></div></dd><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.4.1"><p class="no_margin">
<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.sullivan.2020.1506">Sullivan et al [2020]</a>
</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Autosomal_Dominant_TRPV4Related_Sk"><h3>Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4</i>-Related Skeletal Dysplasias</h3><p>Autosomal dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias have a broad phenotypic spectrum and, thus, there are many skeletal dysplasias to consider in the differential diagnosis.</p><p>The differential diagnosis of <b>mild</b>
<i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias (<a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/familial/">familial</a> digital arthropathy with brachydactyly) includes reactive arthropathy, diabetic arthropathy, and other forms of brachydactyly [<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.hortabaas.2017.512">Horta-Baas et al 2017</a>, <a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF._reldemir.2021.46">&#x000dc;rel-Demir et al 2021</a>].</p><p>The differential diagnosis of <b>intermediate</b>
<i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasias (brachyolmia, spondylometaphyseal dysplasia [Kozlowski type], and spondyloepimetaphyseal dysplasia [Maroteaux type]) and <b>severe</b>
<i>TRPV4-</i>related skeletal dysplasia (metatropic dysplasia) is summarized in <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTintermediateandsevereautosoma">Table 5</a>.</p><div id="cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 5. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p>Intermediate and Severe Autosomal Dominant <i>TRPV4-</i>Related Skeletal Dysplasias: Differential Diagnosis</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" id="__cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma_lrgtbl__"><table class="no_bottom_margin"><thead><tr><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma_1_1_1_1" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Phenotype</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma_1_1_1_2" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Gene</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma_1_1_1_3" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">MOI</th><th id="hd_h_cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma_1_1_1_4" scope="col" rowspan="1" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">Disorder</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td headers="hd_h_cmt2c.T.intermediate_and_severe_autosoma_1_1_1_1" rowspan="6" scope="rowgroup" colspan="1" style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle;">

</p></div></dd><dt>2. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.5.2"><p class="no_margin">
<a class="bk_pop" href="#cmt2c.REF.bownass.2019.1884">Bownass et al [2019]</a>
</p></div></dd><dt>3. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.5.3"><p class="no_margin">Autosomal recessive otospondylomegaepiphyseal dysplasia may also be referred to as Weissenbacher- Zweym&#x000fc;ller syndrome.</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div></div></div><div id="cmt2c.Management"><h2 id="_cmt2c_Management_">Management</h2><p>No clinical practice guidelines for <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related disorders have been published. In the absence of published guidelines, the following recommendations are based on the authors' personal experience managing individuals with this disorder.</p><div id="cmt2c.Evaluations_Following_Initial_Diag"><h3>Evaluations Following Initial Diagnosis</h3><p>To establish the extent of disease and needs in an individual diagnosed with an <b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related neuromuscular disorder</b>, the evaluations summarized in <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4related_neuromuscular_disor/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTtrpv4relatedneuromusculardisor">Table 6a</a> (if not performed as part of the evaluation that led to the diagnosis) are recommended.</p><div id="cmt2c.T.trpv4related_neuromuscular_disor" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 6a. </span></h3><div class="caption"><p><i>TRPV4-</i>Related Neuromuscular Disorder: Recommended Evaluations Following Initial Diagnosis</p></div><p class="large-table-link" style="display:none"><span class="right"><a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.trpv4related_neuromuscular_disor/?report=objectonly" target="object">View in own window</a></span></p><div class="large_tbl" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a>; EMG = electromyography; MOI = <a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/mode-of-inheritance/">mode of inheritance</a>; NCV = nerve conduction velocity; OT = occupational therapy; PT = physical therapy</p></div></dd><dt>1. </dt><dd><div id="cmt2c.TF.6a.1"><p class="no_margin">Clinical geneticist, certified genetic counselor, certified genetic nurse, genetics advanced practice provider (nurse practitioner or physician assistant)</p></div></dd></dl></div></div></div><p>To establish the extent of disease and needs in an individual diagnosed with an <b><a class="def" href="/books/n/gene/glossary/def-item/autosomal-dominant/">autosomal dominant</a> <i>TRPV4</i>-related skeletal dysplasia</b>, the evaluations summarized in <a href="/books/NBK201366/table/cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_3/?report=objectonly" target="object" rid-ob="figobcmt2cTautosomaldominanttrpv4related3">Table 6b</a> (if not performed as part of the evaluation that led to the diagnosis) are recommended.</p><div id="cmt2c.T.autosomal_dominant_trpv4related_3" class="table"><h3><span class="label">Table 6b.
